B00F3VOL38|5.0|It seems like almost every year as Christmas season is about to start they come out with some kind of new Beatles album. Something like a remaster of their albums or rare recordings that had been kept hidden away for years. This year the offering is \\"On Air- Live At The BBC Volume 2\\". It is I guess you would call a follow up to their first \\"Live At The BBC\\" album that was released twenty years ago or so. In any event for those of us who are serious fans of The Beatles this is a must to add to our Beatles collection.<br /><br />As with the first album this is a collection of live performances the Beatles made on BBC radio from around 1962 to 1965. It covers that time in their career when Beatlemania was catching on in England and as the the Lads were preparing to cross the great pond to captivate America. This is the roots of The Beatles music as they were developing their song writing style and sound. They sound fresh and energetic and happy as they are playing their music. This was a time when they enjoyed touring and playing live music before the constant strain and hassles of touring began to wear them down to the point they would finally give up playing concerts and find refuge by going into the recording studio to just make music.<br /><br />The music on Volume 2 is a collection of both The Beatles playing cover versions of other peoples songs as well as performing their own. The sound quality of the music on both discs are excellent with only one or two not quite up to par but still enjoyable to hear. Like with the first volume there is an excellent booklet included that gives a run down on the music being played as well as pictures of John, Paul, George and Ringo. Plus in addition to the songs there are through out the two discs moments of the boys talking either with the announcer of the show they were on or just reading things off of cards that help to show off the wit and charm that all four of The Beatles were known for. There is no doubt I am sure for the listener who is old enough to remember Beatlemania will be nostalgic as they listen to the songs being performed and Lads as they talk. Do know for those readers of this review who have the first BBC album you will notice there are song titles on Volume 2 that are also on Volume 1. These songs however are not repeat recordings. All of the music performed on Volume 2 are different performances from the first album. So get your copy now or add it to you Christmas wish list but for any serious fan of the Beatles this is an album you will want to get.e listener who is old enough to remember Beatlemania will be nostalgic as they listen to the songs being performed and Lads as they talk. 1742204147|5.0|I could quibble here about giving this a five star rating, saying I wanted more photos, and better maps, but this books emphasis, is unlike those comments on my review of the India. I think the detail is easier to get here in-country, or from other sources.  I know a lot of things about Japan from my continued interest in the country since 1998, when I started getting into collecting and watching, anime and manga (Japanese comic books).  It's hard for the zist of the culture, to not seep into that kind of literature and video.  I actually took up an interest in those venues, because I'd seen so much American TV and movies, and growing up as a TV addict since the late fifties, that I've become somewhat jaded, for the lack of a better word. When it comes to knowing American TV and movie plotlines, I could decode the plot in the first few minutes of almost any show.  I noticed this some years ago, when I saw that new movie plots, that were lauded as great and original, by critics and fans alike, were so predictable to me.  Japanese media held a great deal of surprise for me and I became interested in the country as well.  A for instance was a sports anime I watched subtitles called \\"Touch\\", It was about twin brothers and the girl next door.  My Americanized plot radar was decoding this as a love triangle between the brothers and the girl with baseball as the backdrop.  I won't bore you with all the details of the rivalry, but after roughly 25 episodes about the end of the first season of an anime story, over 100 episodes long, one brother is killed in a traffic accident.  A main character, and one primary to what I thought was the main plot, dies and his brothers life changes forever.  Total surprise to me, and why I follow the genre today.<br /><br />This book little regional stories that are told as if they are true, but the astute reader will realize they are just tales about the region to enhance the experience.  For instance, one of the regions tells that it's a hotspot of sorts, for kappa, they explain the humanoid creatures live there, and like to feast on human souls, that they extract from the intestines.  A little gross, but non-the-less true when discussing kappa lore.  It goes on to say, if you go in the water where kappa may be found, to throw some cucumbers as they are a favorite food, and maybe the kappa will leave you alone. If all else fails, since kappa are very Japanese, bow to them and they will return the bow, and let out the water supply they retain in their heads which renders them helpless.  It goes on to say if you feel bad you might replace the water with a bottled water, and the kappa will return the favor in some special way.  Had I not known kappa from Japanese anime, what was in this book, would certainly have enlightened me in an entertaining way.<br /><br />This book is full of other information as well on how to handle visas, getting a rail pass, setting up lodging for a trip in advance, and even some updated sections on the post-Tsunami reconstruction, and the nuclear issues.  I must say if I'd been a much younger person, and could have afforded to, I would have gone there to participate in the reconstruction efforts, it would be a great way to get to know the country and the people.  Most of that is well underway now according to the book, but there are a few areas where people can pitch in an help.  It has the agencies you can contact if interested.<br /><br />As for the nuts and bolts of the book:<br /><br />1.  You have several pre-designed tours, plotted for regions of impressive sites and experiences.<br />2.  The usual good food guides, to either find dining or very interesting eats.<br />3.  A section to tell where to go to avoid the touristy spots if you are very adventurous, or speak some of the language.<br />4.  Tips and details you need to know if you've not visited Japan before or are going to a different region.<br />5.  Sites or experiences highlighted with a star to indicate a can't miss venture.<br />6.  A way to travel on a budget, if you are not rich and want to see more of Japan for less.<br />7.  Lots of small general area maps, that plot the major sites or attractions on them.<br />8.  A survival guide to tell you about post, police, government, medical and other important information.<br />9.  A pull out map of Tokyo (although not as detailed as you'd like).<br />10. Dozens of little history or cultural lessons on anything from fish to beer, to shrines, to the people.<br /><br />Overall, this book contains some of the best information I've ever seen in a Japan guide book.  Sure you can get better maps, and it doesn't include things like apartment info or how to find work, but that is not the intent of the book.  I for one loved hearing there is a Ghibli Museum, as an anime fan, a studio that is oven worshiped and copied by Disney, is some place I'd like to know more about.  Plus, I know a lot of very common cultural evens and such depicted in Japanese anime and manga, and I'd like to experience some of that myself.  Other interesting things to experience might be capsule hotels, and all the crazy vending machines that vend almost anything in Japan.  Either in electronic form, or the paper format this is a book I'll want to take with me on my first trip there, they make a Kindle edition I think, it would be nice to see that one and how well it matches with the paper book.  Recommended, I can't vouch for absolute accuracy, but the book does appear to strive for it as much as possible.  While I doubt everything is changed completely from the last pre-Tsunami edition, they've updated a lot so you won't be too misled.  If you follow the advice and book lodging well in advance you'll run into few troubles.  One thing I learned from this book, is that Japan is not a credit card culture, nor is it a place where hotels are just booked on the fly or walk-ins.  Plan accordingly.

0804137501|5.0|I'm a big fan of the SvN blog and Rework and Getting Real, so it's safe to say I was excited about Remote when I heard about it earlier this year. Coming from a global professional services firm, we've adopted a lot of workplace flexibility measures to fight back on the expenses associated with office space and our high-turnover/stress environment. I share the authors belief that going remote is the way of the future and this book highlights the reasons for going remote, the arguments against, and methods for optimizing the value of a remote culture.<br /><br />Since I work in IT, I understand the obvious connection between the authors and the software business. I think they did a fine job showing why remote working isn't just for IT and why there are benefits across industries. One of the few shortcomings of Rework was that I thought it was a little too IT-centric; some of the arguments presented there just wouldn't fit well in non-IT, traditional (read: old-fashioned) businesses. Remote does a good job explaining why some of these more traditional old-fashioned businesses can benefit from going remote. I especially liked the sections about hiring and retaining the best employees. Everyone can relate to this and it has helpful that they went into detail to explain exactly how they do it at 37 Signals (since at this point they are pretty good at it).<br /><br />It's a short, enjoyable read. It leaves the reader with a sense of what work could be like if you're courageous enough to think outside the norm and challenge conventions as to what work is supposed to be (and what it can be). I'd argue that almost any business owner or decision maker could benefit from this book, even if for just one little bit of information that makes you follow-up with one of their sourced documents. I know that finishing this book is just the beginning for me to rethink how remote work arrangements can be optimized.

B00EW9GQR6|3.0|I'm one of the biggest fans of Gaga out there, and after waiting for an album that was constantly hyped for two years by Gaga and her team as nothing short of the rebirth of pop music, I can't even begin to explain how disappointed I am in this album. I'm used to Gaga constantly reinventing herself and her music, and that's usually what makes me such a big fan, because she always manages to pull it off. I was amazed at how effortlessly she transitioned between the light-heartedness of The Fame to the dark and edgy Fame Monster to the completely rock n' roll Born this Way. But ARTPOP is the first of her albums where the transition to a new style did not pay off.<br /><br />What exactly is the style and genre of music in ARTPOP that's so disappointing, then? Well, if you figure it out, let me know. Because after about a dozen listens, I still see the album as just a mix tape of different genres that have all been pummeled into submission to fit into the scope of pop songs, doing service to neither the genre in question nor pop music. The way that Gaga blended rock and pop in Born this Way was seamless and nothing short of brilliance. In ARTPOP, however, the hands of the craftswoman show, and the songs in result sound contrived and try-hard. It seems that rather than just trying to make decent pop music as she has all her career (and succeeding, for the most part), she decided instead to imprint this artificial idea of \\"ART\\" (in all caps, of course, to make it even more pretentious) onto songs that otherwise might have succeeded.<br /><br />In some cases, the experimentation does pay off, such as in the blistering middle-eastern inspired opener track, \\"Aura\\", although if you can find the demo version on the internet, I recommend downloading that one instead, because Gaga managed to ruin even one of the more successful songs on this album by changing the first verse into an alien-esque drawl that is just plain painful to listen to. \\"Do What U Want\\" featuring R. Kelly was also one of the big surprises of the album. The collaboration, strange at first, turned out to be pop brilliance. The song sounds like Gaga's take on a Michael Jackson song, updated for today's audiences, and the risk she took paid off big time.<br /><br />The worst offenders on this album are the ones that try too hard. And it's a shame, because listening to them, you can see an unpolished gem at the center of them. And if you could follow me in this metaphor for a moment, it's painfully obvious that these gems were never meant to be displayed as they are on the album. They needed a hard cut down the middle, to become something entirely different, because they were never going to work in the first place without a major revision. But instead, it feels like Gaga just kept on polishing and polishing them into something they were never meant to be in the first place.<br /><br />Prime examples are \\"Manicure\\", which has a delicious grinding 50s beat, but a chorus that goes nowhere and lyrics that run aimlessly around a forced pun, and \\"Venus\\", which tries so hard to be experimental and pop at the same time, yet ends up sounding like I accidentally hit the \\"next\\" button on my ipod several times during the song by accident, landing on several 80s synth songs that should've stayed back in the 80s. \\"Jewels N Drugs\\" is quite simply an abomination. Gaga tries so hard to be hood, but it's just glaringly obvious that this is a privileged girl who grew up on the upper west side and isn't in touch with hip hop as a genre whatsoever.<br /><br />In the end, the songs sound as pretentious and try-hard as the title. The album gives one the feeling that Gaga is trying so hard to prove to the world that she's a true \\"artiste\\", and not just another pop star, but the joke ends up being on her, because the world already knows she is, even if she doesn't. The ending track \\"Applause\\" truly is perfect for this album, because it encapsulates what the album is silently screaming in every second -- that this is a work craving validation from the world rather than validating itself and waiting for the world to follow, as Gaga's previous albums did. The hype has become more important than the work, and in the end, the album is as colossal yet lifeless as the sculpture on the cover.

B000SSPL2Q|5.0|This box set is the sine qua non of Sibelius box sets.  Neeme Jarvi and the Gothenburg Symphony capture the coolness and ethereality of Sibelius perfectly.  There is no Romantic excess or false lushness here, as you can perceive in the von Karajan recordings.  Jarvi coaxes a crisp precision from the Gothenburg musicians, producing a sound reminiscent of the Cleveland Orchestra under George Szell in the 1960s. Out of all the superhumans in the marvel universe thus far, Loki is by far the most human.<br /><br />Thor is an oaf who's idea of fun is literally pounding his way through things.  At the beginning especially, he is completely ruled by his ego and emotions. This changes somewhat through the course of the movie.  Though not necessarily stupid, Thor is not really intelligent either.  His idea of war includes no forethought and no strategy. In fact, if it weren't for Loki it would've been a bit of a throw-a-way like most other movies of this genre.  Chris Hemsworth makes a great Thor, but the script fails him sometimes.  The romance between Thor and Jane is difficult to swallow.  The idea that a brilliant physicist falls head over heels for a witless oaf like Thor (as he can be described in the beginning of the movie)  just because he fills out his shirt well is a hard sell and unfortunately, this film doesn't even bother to try to sell it.  Thor's interest in Jane is also a bit of a mystery as they have nothing in common. We're simply asked to accept this romance without ever being given any reason to care whether or not they come together - or stay together for that matter.  Some scenes were choppy.  Though not a perfect movie, it is by far the best superhero movie I've personally seen to date.

Casting directly means that, once you select a movie/program/video, the selecting device (e.g. tablet/smartphone/computer) can be turned off. This streams directly to the Chromecast module, as fast as the network connection allows. You can also stream from a chrome browser tab, but when you do this, the browser tab actually runs the video, and the Chromecast module taps into that stream. And, you can't stream from Chrome running on a tablet or phone, only from a computer. This is inconvenient. Consider:<br /><br /> In 1969 the Who released the original two-LP rock opera.<br /><br /> In 1970 the Who released an EP featuring selections from Tommy.<br /><br /> In 1970 the Who released an orchestral version of Tommy with London Symphony Orchestra and a bevy of rock stars.<br /><br /> In 1975 Ken Russell directed the movie Tommy.<br /><br /> In 1975 the soundtrack LP from the movie was released.<br /><br /> In 1990 Mobile Fidelity Sound Lab released a 24 K gold CD of Tommy.<br /><br /> In 1993 the Who remastered Tommy and released it on cassette.<br /><br /> In 1996 the Who brought Tommy to the Broadway stage. An LP of course was released afterwards.<br /><br /> In 1996 the Who released a remastered version of Tommy.<br /><br /> In 2004 the Who released a Deluxe version of Tommy.<br /><br /> In 2007 the Who released the Deluxe version of  Tommy in SACD.<br /><br /> In 2013 the Who released Tommy 2013 Deluxe, with songs substantially similar to and containing the same songs and alternative versions as the previous Deluxe version(s).<br /><br /> Now there is ... (drum roll) ... the Super Deluxe Tommy, three CDs with a Blue Ray DVD.<br /><br />Enough already!! What is the point ??<br /><br />Tommy is a masterful composition and will withstand the test of time  -- and will continue to do so, or at least as long as Pete Townshend is alive to re-market Tommy in yet another version. The continual re-packaging of Tommy serves only to diminish the value of this great musical piece. True, between 1975 and 1996 the Who did not re-released Tommy. During that time period Townshend could have, but didn't, release a disco version of Tommy. We must all thank him for that.<br /><br />Even with all the extras in this combo this release reeks of rank consumerism. Most of the songs are excellent quality and they include lots of early hits as well as great cover versions, showing how versatile the Beatles were and giving you an idea of the set list they would have played live in Liverpool and Hamburg. The poorest audio is heard on John singing Chuck Berry's, \\"I'm talking about you,\\" which was presumably worth including because of its rarity. There are lots of early tracks on here which the Beatles never recorded and they give you an excellent flavour of the band in those early days. 0618918248|1.0|This will be an informal, unrevised review, as I do not think this book is worth much more.<br /><br />I do not really understand how this book is a best-seller. But that is perhaps due to my own personality as a lover of non-fiction works rather than sensationalist literature as this. I found this book in the \\"Science\\" section at Barnes & Nobles stores, despite it makes arguments in ethics, philosophy, history, and theology but hardly any science. Dawkins is no \\"expert\\" or \\"scholar\\" on virtually anything he asserts in \\"The God Delusion\\" (GD for short).<br /><br />The fact Dawkins continuously boasts in GD to represent some purely scientific, unbiased opinion is laughable. If you need proof Dawkins is about as emotional as a writer can get on a topic, just read his notice on the Old Testament:<br /><br />\\"The God of the Old Testament is arguably the most unpleasant character in all fiction: jealous and proud of it; a petty, unjust, unforgiving control-freak; a vindictive, bloodthirsty ethnic cleanser; a misogynistic, homophobic, racist, infanticidal, genocidal, filicidal, pestilential, megalomaniacal, sadomasochistic, capriciously malevolent bully.\\"<br />(GD, 118, iBooks version)<br /><br />Because this is exactly how an unbiased observer who merely arrived at critical, sound conclusions speaks (yes that was sarcasm)...I wonder how long Dawkins sat around thinking the amount of name-calling language he could use for God. In any event, this language is clearly more emotional than rational.<br /><br />GD claims to show how belief in God is wrong, yet the majority of the book is concerned with the ethical conduct of religion and religion's moral worth, not whether God exists. Dawkins only briefly goes into philosophical arguments for God's existence and only gives the most basic, rudimentary versions of them a philosophy grad as myself know are practically strawman representations of the true debate. There are far more elaborate and in-depth arguments Dawkins is obviously oblivious too. But perhaps just like Theology, he considers Philosophy a useless discipline next to science. This leads to my main point on a field I have read much in and know Dawkins does not know what he is talking about: Theology and/or biblical history.<br /><br />Dawkins only cites ONE biblical scholar for his claims on the origins of Christianity, the historical Jesus, and so forth: Bart Ehrman. Of course, Ehrman himself would scarcely agree with Dawkins in how much he uses the Gospels as important sources in his \\"Jesus Apocalyptic Prophet of the New Millenium\\" (decent book btw and I doubt Dawkins read it) for example. But Dawkins makes strong claims with usually no citation or sources like these:<br /><br />\\"During the Roman occupation of Palestine, Christianity was founded by Paul of Tarsus as a less ruthlessly monotheistic sect of Judaism and a less exclusive one, which looked outwards from the Jews to the rest of the world.\\" (140)<br /><br />\\"In any case, as I said, there is no good historical evidence that he ever thought he [Jesus] was divine.\\" (314)<br /><br />\\"Ever since the nineteenth-century, scholarly theologians have made an overwhelming case that the gospels are not reliable accounts of what happened in the history of the real world. All were written long after the death of Jesus, and also after the epistles of Paul, which mention almost none of the alleged facts of Jesus' life. All were then copied and recopied, through many different 'Chinese Whispers generations' by fallible scribes who, in any case, had their own religious agendas.\\" (314-315)<br /><br />\\"The four gospels that made it into the official canon were chosen, more or less arbitrarily, out of a larger sample of at least a dozen including the Gospel of Thomas, Peter, Nicodemus, Philip, Bartholomew and Mary Magdalen.\\" (324)<br /><br />\\"Although Jesus probably existed, reputable biblical scholars do not in general regard the New Testament (and obviously not the Old Testament) as areliable record of what actually happened in history, and I shall not consider the Bible further as evidence for any kind of deity.\\" (328)<br /><br />Out of all those claims, the only one with a source is the fourth, and that is again only from Ehrman, the only scholar Dawkins seems to know about. Ehrman himself is hardly a renowned scholar as he is made out to believe in the media. Though he is an authority in textual criticism, he is no often cited and known authority in the scholarly community for the debates surrounding the historical Jesus and early church. There, names like James D.G. Dunn, N.T. Wright, John P. Meier, E.P. Sanders, and many others are far more known and cherished works. Most scholars like Dunn don't agree with Ehrman on his very cliché view of oral tradition in the ancient world being similar to the Telephone Game or Chinese Whispers:<br /><br />\\"In both cases, however, my main criticism is that these theories of memory have been framed, once again, in a literary culture. They do not take sufficient account of the differences that might have been and almost certainly were involved in an oral culture. In a culture that could not rely on widespread literacy to disseminate wisdom or to propagate particular ideas, where memory was much more trained to the retention of important information, where skills had been developed in society and among groups to ensure the preservation of memories important to these groups, the dynamic of memory was bound to be different.\\" (James D.G. Dunn, A New Perspective on Jesus, pp. 45)<br /><br />Therefore, the only place where Dawkins relies on a source is the only source he knows and, unsurprisingly, shows his ignorance of the actual debates in NT scholarship. As for all the other quotes, he never cites a source and most of them are so wrong, I do not need anymore proof this man never bothers to research. As a history grad, as well, I certainly know such claims without a footnote or endnote or any kind of citationwould earn a paper with them a failing grade in any college-level history course. Historians prize themselves on citations which is often not only a science in citing sources and being critical, but is even treated as another essential part of historical writing for aesthetic reasons. A genuine history book will not lack a plethora of notes and bibliography. Yet on such grand claims like most theologians do not consider the Gospels reliable deserves multiple sources cited. Dawkins gives zero. So to live up to my word, though the \\"nineteenth-century\\" biblical scholarship was overtly skeptical, contemporary biblical scholarship is extremely more positive about the Gospels:<br /><br />\\"Research in the historical Jesus has taken several positive steps in recent years. Archaeology, remarkable literary discoveries, such as the Dead Sea Scrolls, and progress in reassessing the social, economic, and political setting of first-century Palestine have been major factors....the persistent trend in recent years is to see the Gospels as essentially reliable, especially when properly understood, and to view the historical Jesus in terms much closer to Christianity's traditional understanding, i.e., as the proclaimer of God's rule, as understanding himself as the Lord's anointed, and, indeed, as God's own son, destined to rule Israel.\\" (Craig Evans, \\"Assessing Progress in the Third Quest of the Historical Jesus,\\" Journal for the Study of the Historical Jesus 4.1 (2006): 54)<br /><br />Finally, what is strange about refuting all of this is Dawkins prior to trying to assert what biblical scholars and \\"theologians\\" believe actually claimed Theology itself is a useless discipline:<br /><br />\\"I am tempted to go further and wonder in what possible sense theologians can be said to have (italics) a province. I am still amused when I recall the remark of a former Warden (head) of my Oxford college. A young theologian had applied for a junior research fellowship, and his doctoral thesis onChristian theology provoked the Warden to say, 'I have grave doubts as to whether it's a subject (italcs) at all.' What expertise can theologians bring to deep cosmological questions that scientists cannot? In another book I recounted the words of an Oxford astronomer who, when I asked him one of those same deep questions, said: 'Ah, now we move beyond the realm of science. This is where I have to hand over to our good friend the chaplain.' I was not quick-witted enough to utter the response that I later wrote: 'But why the chaplain? Why not the gardener or the chef?' What are scientists so cravenly respectful towards the ambitions of theologians, over questions that theologians are certainly no more qualified to answer than scientists themselves?\\" (202-203)<br /><br />And yet...he proudly boasts later \\"scholarly theologians have made an overwhelming case that the gospels are not reliable accounts of what happened in the history of the real world.\\"<br /><br />Perhaps the reason why he utterly failed to portray the accurate debate among theologians and biblical scholars now is because he does not even think they are worth his time unless they utter something he thinks agrees with his dogma (for dogma it is). Then he announces it everywhere completely ignorant of the surrounding context to it. As I said, certainly nineteenth-century to the early twentieth century theologians ruled by very skeptical German theologians like Rudolf Bultmann believed practically nothing in the Gospels is historical. But today? Not even close. Though I would not say theologians, biblical scholars, and historians related to Jesus and early Christianity are in agreement on traditional Christian doctrines of Jesus being correct, they are certainly not that skeptical anymore. Dawkins may be right that most scholars do not LITERALLY BELIEVE Jesus was divine, but most scholars do believe Jesus, at least, believed he was a divine being in some sense (perhaps not God himself for some scholars though). The very agnostic and skeptical scholar Dale Allison Jr. even admitted:<br /><br />\\"No follower of Jesus, to our knowledge, ever called Paul divine or reckoned him a god. Christians did, however, say astounding things about Jesus, and that from the very beginning. The differing evaluations, I submit, had something to do with who those two people actually were. We should hold a funeral for the view that Jesus entertained no exalted thoughts about himself.\\" (Dale C. Allison, Constructing Jesus: Memory, Imagination, and History, pp. 304)<br /><br />Most scholars today also believe Jesus even worked unexplainable wonders like miraculous healings:<br /><br />\\"In sum, the statement that Jesus acted as and was viewed as an exorcist and healer during his public ministry has as much historical corroboration as almost any other statement we can make about the Jesus of history. Indeed, as a global affirmation about Jesus and his ministry it has much better attestation than many other assertions made about Jesus, assertions that people often take for granted. Looming large in the Gospels and no doubt in his actual ministry, Jesus' miracle-working activity played an integral part in his being able to attract attention, both positive and negative. His miracle-working activity not only supported but also dramatized and actuated his eschatological message, and it may have contributed to some degree to the alarm felt by the authorities who finally brought about his death. Any historian who seeks to portray the historical Jesus without giving due weight to his fame as a miracle-worker is not delineating this strange and complex Jew, but rather a domesticated Jesus reminiscent of the bland moralist created by Thomas Jefferson.\\" (John P. Meier, A Marginal Jew: Rethinking the Historical Jesus, Vol. 2, pp. 970)<br /><br />\\"There is an increasingly large consensus that the historical Jesus can be credited with working miracles of some kind, even if only of the more easily accepted exorcisms and healings, rather than the spectacular mighty works such as feeding multitudes and raising the dead. Further, it is possible to make a case that the performing of miracles was an important part of the pre-Easter ministry of the historical Jesus.\\" (Graham H. Twelftree, \\"The Miracles of Jesus: Marginal or Mainstream?\\" Journal for the Study of the Historical Jesus 1.1 (2003): 104-105)<br /><br />Of course an ardent skeptic like Dawkins would NEVER wish to tell his audience MOST of his supposed scholars challenge his own naturalistic worldview that miracles do not occur, hence he is willfully ignorant about it and mocks Theology and any discipline related to it as a waste of time. Then hopes no one catches the contradiction when he suddenly employs it when he thinks it agrees with his worldview.<br /><br />In the end, these are really his only pages dedicated to the history of the Bible, which is the only other field related to religious truth he engages in to an extent beyond philosophical proofs. He has very brief arguments in Philosophy. But, as I said at the beginning, most of \\"The God Delusion\\" is about ethics in and of religion, not trying to dismantle arguments for God's existence or a religion like Christianity's truth. In that light, GD really already assumes the title and claim of the book is correct, dedicating far more pages under the assumption Dawkins is already right rather than trying to PROVE he is right. \\"The God Delusion\\" is a book believed only by the gullible such as Dawkins.

0385341008|4.0|I'm a 14 year old who read this book for my history class.<br />The Year is 1946,London is trying to balance itself after the impact of WWII. As writer Juliet Ashton is struggling to find a subject for her next book, she gets an unexpected letter from a man she has never met named Dawsey Adams. Juliet finds out that this man is a native to the island Guernsey, and came across her name and address in one of her old books she had by Charles Lamb. Juliet is immediately drawn into a new world when Dawsey mentions The Guernsey Literary and Potato Peel Pie Society in his letters. (Originally meant for its members to get a roast pig dinner, but later was found as a fun and pleasant way to pass the time during the German Occupation). When Juliet and Dawsey keep writing to one another, she finds out more about the island and about the charming, funny, and lively people living there. When Juliet starts writing to the other members of the society, she notices how much the occupation affected Guernsey, she is desperate to learn more and embarks on a journey she will never leave.<br />This novel composed entirely of letters written by Mary Ann and Annie Barrows was a great educational read.  It was a \\"none gory\\" way to learn about the occupation while still being very educational.  I learned so much about the time period, and had fun just reading it.  Both authors really made this book come alive.  I give it 4 stars.  Some swearing in it, but a clean read otherwise.

B008J8KBKY|5.0|This is definitely a must have for anyone who is a fan of Connect Four. It's the same old game you know in love with a few cool twists, and it's ideal for both single player and multiplayer.<br /><br />Four in a 3d Row is, as the name suggests, a three dimensional game, so instead of playing on a square board, you're playing on a cube. You place your pieces on various nodes instead of dropping chips into a slot, so you can go anywhere on the board. You still connect four in a row vertically, horizontally, and diagonally along the surface area of the cube, but with the 3d you have a lot more places to go, which can drag out the game in the higher levels of difficulty.<br /><br />This game has a sort of RPG style level up system to it. You start at level one, and you earn experience points for winning games and unlocking achievements. There are four different levels of difficulty; each one has to be unlocked by winning one game on the previous difficulty level. After you earn 20,000 points, you have the option of setting turn-based time limits to add a little bit of difficulty.<br /><br />Now, here's what earns this game a good rating from me. This app comes with the permissions necessary for multiplayer gameplay, and you do have to create an account to participate. However, the devs do it right for this game. The entire online multiplayer part is optional, meaning that internet access is not a requirement to play this game. With online multiplayer enabled, you can access the leader board and see how you stack up to other players, but offline mode does keep track of your personal score. In addition, you can still play multiplayer offline with your friends in person as well as playing the computer in single player mode.<br /><br />With all of that being said, here's my usual breakdown:<br /><br />Gameplay: 5/5 - The 3d board adds a lot more fun and challenge to this game. There's also a tutorial at the beginning which can be accessed again from the main menu. Like I said before, there is single player and multiplayer gameplay available online and offline.<br /><br />Interface: 5/5 - The graphics are brilliant for a game of its caliber, and the controls are smooth and responsive. Personally, I would prefer a bit more of a soundtrack to this game, but that's not a very big deal.<br /><br />Permissions: 5/5 - Permissions are reasonable for this sort of game, as networking is needed for online multiplayer gameplay as well as the leader board. There is nothing obtrusive, no IAP, no ads - I'm quite pleased with this one.<br /><br />Overall, this is a solid 5 star app in my book, and I definitely recommend getting this FAOTD.<br /><br />- Sam

1250030951|4.0|Rainbow Rowell's latest doesn't stray too far from the tropes of teen coming-of-age romance, but it carries them out with enough subtlety and humor that even readers averse to the form will be able to have fun with it. It helps that there's a lot more going on than just romantic angst. Cath has been living an isolated but happy existence: she has her devoted but troubled father, her twin sister Wren, and her sort-of boyfriend Abel, but her biggest bond is with Simon Snow, a series of teen fantasy novels (think Harry Potter). Cath is the author of \\"Carry On, Simon,\\" a very popular fanfiction alternative to the as-yet-unreleased final book in the series. She has no trouble writing the relationship between Simon and his archenemy Baz (think Harry/Draco slash), but real relationships are tougher, which is why she spends most of her first weeks at college sitting in her dorm room and eating protein bars rather than brave the dining hall. But new opportunities are everywhere: in the upper-level fiction writing class she's taking, in the roommate who may not be as forbidding as she appears, and in two guys who are much more appealing than reliable but unexciting Abel. Will Cath spread her wings and discover life beyond Simon Snow?<br /><br />Well, obviously. But this isn't a \\"stop being a nerd and have a real life\\" book. Nor is it a \\"romance delayed by ridiculous contrivances\\" book, or an \\"all her problems solved by the final page\\" book. Cath doesn't have to abandon Simon Snow, only balance it with other kinds of pleasure. It doesn't take very long for her to figure out which of her two romantic prospects is the right one, and while there's an unnecessary hiccup on the way to happiness, it resolves pretty quickly. And Cath's family life integrates real issues (a manic-depressive father, an absent mother, a sister too into partying) that neither degenerate into melodrama nor turn out to have easy answers. Cath feels like a real teenager rather than a stereotypical socially awkward fangirl. Her eventual boyfriend is a little too good to be true, but not objectionably so. Dialogue that's clever but no so much so as to feel unnatural in the mouths of college students is the final element in a novel that pushes a formula about as far as it can go. FANGIRL doesn't break any new ground or offer stunning insights into fandom, but it's a reasonably realistic and enjoyable teen novel all the same.awkward fangirl. Martin.<br /><br />The character development, world building, and story arc all suck you in and make it nearly impossible to put it down. Sanderson is excellent not only because of his vision and his knack for storytelling (which are both considerable), he is excellent because he holds himself to a standard of quality in writing that is nearly unparalleled. He does multiple revisions - complete revisions - on each piece he writes. That means after completing the story - reaching a point where other authors might say, &#34;Okay, let's send this off to the publisher!&#34; - Sanderson goes back through and revises multiple times. Fantasy novels as a genre would be greatly improved if more authors had Sanderson's work ethic and expectation of quality in their own work. B0084U0IEG|4.0|When is a spy novel not a spy novel? When it’s in the hands of the excellent Ian McEwen, who uses his prodigious writing skill to give us a literary, and very literate, tale of love and betrayal in early 1970s England. Much time is spent in Sweet Tooth recreating a world of striking miners, IRA bombings and an oil embargo that’s disrupting the already fragile British economy. But, for the characters in the book, not much actually “happens,” as more time is spent in contemplation and reflection than their actual movement. But this is not, believe it or not, a bad thing, unless one is expecting to read the kind of novel that would be written by Tom Clancy, for example.<br />We’re brought into this richly-detailed world by Serena Frome (&#34;rhymes with plume,&#34; she tells us),who lives in the present day, and announces, with the book’s first sentences, that she will be recounting what occurred in her life forty-years earlier. Serena was a beautiful and bright young woman who was seduced into a career at MI5 by an older lover with heavy secrets in his past. She was at first only a lowly clerk, and occasionally a safe-house cleaning lady, with a tendency to fall in love with every significant man in her life. She also had a love for literature, or at least read a lot of paperback novels, and on this basis she is given an unusual assignment: MI5 is funding a literary foundation which will discover and support young pro-Western writers, in order to counteract the heavy cultural blitz coming out of communist Russia. Serena must sign up Tom Haley, just such a writer, without ever letting on where the sponsorship money from the foundation is coming from, especially since, for the most part, the British artistic community of the day was left-leaning.<br />That’s pretty much it as far as the espionage aspect of the novel, so any reader must know that going in. However, if you loved McEwen’s Atonement, as I did, you will surely enjoy his perceptive commentary on British society in the 1970s, his often heartbreaking descriptions of Serena’s many loves, and his biting portrayal of the lies and deceptions that go into so many human relationships. Essentially, what this book is about are those very deceptions. Betrayals of Queen and country, yes, but even worse betrayals of lovers and friends.<br />This is also a book about books, with the names of British literary lions and their many works bandied about casually among the characters, who are left to ask what role literature can play, or should play, in indoctrinating the public in one political system or the other. The reading choices of the various characters, whether they prefer books that are classic or just trendy, famous or obscure, also serve to reveal their true selves and their hidden desires. This is truly a book for book lovers.<br />If I had any complaint it was with the ending that was more clever than heartfelt, containing a circular device which makes clear why the narrator is in the present and looking back on events that unfolded in the early 1970s. Unfortunately, I felt in the book’s final pages that the characters that I’d come to care about were not behaving as real people would have, but merely in a way that would allow for the clever ending. This was certainly not such a turn off as to ruin what was otherwise a very enjoyable book, but it did make me enjoy it just a little bit less.on British society in the 1970s, his often heartbreaking descriptions of Serena’s many loves, and his biting portrayal of the lies and deceptions that go into so many human relationships. B00F8KIJ2I|5.0|This is the best Avril album in a long while--maybe her best ever. In fact, I think so. Here, you get every version of Avril. You get the toe-tapping teen anthem (17). You get the defiant, frequently foul-mouthed Princess of Punk (Rock 'n' Roll; Here's to Never Growing Up). You get some things that remind you of her teen affectations in those songs and some others (and talking about her teen affectations, take a look at that awful cover photo...). Well, here's to never growing up, I guess, but that sure isn't all that's here--and by the way, I rather liked those three songs a lot. They are rock with melody, not just noise, something she always did well. There are plenty of toe tappers (You ain't seen nothing yet; Bitchin' Summmer; Sippin' on Sunshine; and some of the aforementioned). You get up tempo ballads (like Hello, Heartache), which straddle the middle of the road.    Then, it changes--as if to say, \\"I'm still me, I still rock and don't you forget it, but I can do more than that.\\"    There's also a new vision of Avril here. You also get the sultry, sexpot torch singer (Give You What You Like). How is that song not the biggest hit in the country? Too sexy for the prudish, I guess. Our Punk Princess is all grown up! There is the big, lush duet \\"Let Me Go.\\" Then, there are the sweet, melodic ballads at the end (the charming Falling Fast; the beautiful Hush Hush). This is an Avril that has a direction for the future. She's spreading her wings, brandishing her skills as a songwriter and flexing her muscles as a singer. She's wasn't ever one dimensional, but the mastery of different genres is pretty clear now.    Really, there's only 1 weak song on the whole album--the inane and puerile Hello, Kitty, made for Japan. What in the world was she thinking? Especially since, as an artist she is demonstrating growth, remarkable talent and serious songwriting skill here. Let's not mar that picture.    This was not as commercially successful as it should've been. I know there were some hits here and overall the album certainly did fairly well compared to most artists, but says Wikipedia, as an album \\"it also tallied the lowest first-week sales of Lavigne's career.\\"  That is a shame (but at least it didn't actually tank; still went top 5). There are probably more great and well-written songs here than on any of her other albums and also more maturity on display as a songwriter on most of them. Just shake it off, Avril...a sly reference because...as I write this, I can't help but notice something--another young female singer who is dominating the charts with far less reason. I can't help but make the comparison. This is way, way better all around, better written, more real, more melodic, better sung and showing far more creative skill in all phases. The songs are memorable and distinctive.

Its very portable but i own a newer tv (about a year old) and it met the requirement for being able to power the chromecast without using the usb but i lucked out and it still needs the usb power supply, no big deal. This is a great buy for $35 and i've read many reviews that compared it to an apple tv (which i also own) but after using it for a few days i can't see how this is comparable. People compare \\"casting\\" to \\"airplay\\" and the two aren't the same.  When you \\"cast\\" something it only displays whats in the tab and when you go full screen (for a video thats not netflix or youtube) it can't do anything else since the full screen, well... fills the screen and it only mirrors at 720. For \\"airplay\\" when you want to display something you just extend the screen drag it over and when you go full screen it still allows you to use your mac and it will be in the same resolution as your tv (assuming your not using a 4k tv). Also airplay makes the phone into a controller for certain iphone games. So to compare the two seems silly to me. Also the apple tv has the apps built in and has a better interface than any phone. Yet for the price i love the chromecast, its cheap, simple and very portable but its definitely not going to replace my apple tv.<br /><br />I gave it 3 stars since i can't go full screen without losing the ability to do anything else on my mac and since it only mirrors at 720 but again i only bought it to use for traveling and maybe on the bedroom tv. Google is a great company so i'm sure that these issues will go away with time and when they do i will change my review but as of now it is what it is.

This paperback literally looks (and feels) like a brick.<br /><br />Ken Follett does a great job at making the bad guys truly villainous.  I was really rooting for their demise throughout the book.  The good guys, however, did not seem as full developed.  I was still glad when they had a good turn, but I did not connect with any of them.  Nevertheless, I do like stories in which there are several characters that intertwine throughout the story.  Ken Follett did that, and it is a feat to tell the stories of 4+ characters over a 30+ time span.  So, I would give this book a 3.5 rating overall - Not a waste of time, but not a wonderful read either.

B00E8S2KV2|5.0|Man, I love this movie. Unfortunately I haven't seen it in about a year, because I didn't want to purchase the DVD until the extended edition came out. But it was definitely worth the wait!<br /><br />The extended/new scenes are great bonuses, just as they were in LOTR (I actually hadn't seen the theatrical versions of LOTR until after I had seen the extended editions about a billion times, and I always felt like so much was missing). It's perfectly understandable why these were not included in the theatrical version, but I'm so glad they were put in for the extended editions.<br /><br />Of course, similar to the movie, if one is expecting the book of The Hobbit exactly, you'll be disappointed. But what I love about Peter Jackson's Hobbit is that he explores many details of Tolkien's world and tries to bridge the gap between The Hobbit and LOTR. Is it necessary? Not at all, or else Tolkien would have done it that way. But I do think it's incredibly fun and interesting to see things fleshed out more. I know the story of the book well, so it's just great to be able to see Jackson's adaptation, including the addition of things created by Tolkien and things Jackson and his team created--again, it is, after all, an adaptation, and in my mind, a great one.<br /><br />A brief breakdown of the extended scenes:<br /><br />-More of Erebor. You get to see a little more of Erebor and its wealth, as well as more tension between the Dwarves and the Elves--an offering of gems is made to King Thranduil, but taken away once he tries to take it.<br /><br />-Slightly more of Smaug. Not much at all, and I didn't even notice this edition until I read it on another site, but you do see an extremely quick flash of his silhouetted body. Which, on that note, this is one thing that I never minded when seeing the movie--a lot of people wanted to see more of Smaug, but I actually liked this choice better. I definitely wanted to see more of Smaug because of how excited I was to see him, but inthis scene, the unseen Smaug works better, in my opinion. But to each his own.<br /><br />-More of Hobbiton. This was one of my favorite additions--we get to see quite a bit more of Hobbiton (which is now permanently built into the hills in New Zealand--I definitely need to visit that before I die). We see a party much like Bilbo's birthday party in Fellowship but this time Bilbo is a child, and at one point hits Gandalf with a wooden sword, which is completely adorable. Later, you see Biblo walking through the marketplace in Hobbiton as he's shopping and trying to avoid Gandalf. I love Hobbiton, and having these scenes was great.<br /><br />-Probably more of the Dwarves in Bilbo's house. I couldn't tell you what they were, but the scene did feel slightly longer--perhaps just my imagination.<br /><br />-A lot more of Rivendell. All very short scenes, but there's quite a few of them. There's more of the Dwarves eating, including one of my now-favorite scenes where Bofur stands up on the table (quite rudely) and starts singing a pub song (which is actually a song from The Fellowship of the Ring that Frodo sings, who says he learned it from Bilbo. I believe it's Jackson who explains in the Appendices that it's up to the viewer to decide if Bilbo learned it after Bofur sung it, or if Bilbo had taught it to Bofur before--I like both ideas, really). I just love songs like this, and hope to see more in the next two movies. You also see more of Bilbo exploring Rivendell (including looking at the image of Sauron fighting Isuldur behind the shards of Narsil that you see in Fellowship). You also hear Elrond and Gandalf discuss the quest, and the White Council scene is extended as well.<br /><br />-The Goblin King. This is quite possibly my favorite new scene, because the Great Goblin sings a song based on the text in the book. It really adds a bit to his (and the goblins as a whole) character, and on top of that it's just a really fun song. It's out of tune and very goblin-y and torture-filled, but I still loved it. \\"Down in the Deep of Goblin Town.\\" There's various other small bits added to the goblin scenes, too.<br />There may have been a little more of the pale orc, but I can't remember anything specifically. Probably a few other small scenes I missed.<br /><br />I'm really only disappointed with two things:<br /><br />1. That the \\"Riddles in the Dark\\" were not extended. There's more in the book, and I completely understand only having the ones they did in the theatrical version, but I just wish more had been added in the extended edition. This was my single favorite scene of the whole movie (I mean seriously, Andy Serkis just did such a supurbly amazing and fantastic job--Martin Freeman, too), and I would love to have seen a longer version. Ah, well; and<br /><br />2. I wish that the \\"Misty Mountains\\" song was extended. The song in the book is much longer, and I just absolutely love the melody in the movie. I wish they could have made it longer.<br /><br />One thing that I thought was a little funny was how the extended edition is kind of similar to the Fellowship extended editions. Both mark their half-way points (Disk 2) in Rivendell, both have the cast running from goblins in the second half of the film, both start with Hobbiton. . . . Not a bad thing at all, IMO, but yeah.<br /><br />Overall, I love the extended scenes in An Unexpected Journey, and I still love the movie as a whole. I still couldn't help to feel that some of the time the digital effects were a little much. I'm not sure exactly how to explain it, but LOTR just felt so real to me, and in The Hobbit, some things seem more obviously digital. Maybe it's just nostalgia or something, but yeah.<br /><br />The movie also seems to have a constant struggle between being a kid's movie and not. The movie is by far much darker than the book, which I didn't mind, but there's also moments that it seems so close to the feel of the book that it feels much more kid-ish. LOTR seemed to have a much more consistent tone throughout the movie. It had light-hearted (Hobbiton, for example) and comedic moments, yet those scenes didn't seem to change the overall tone at all as it sometimes did in The Hobbit.<br /><br />I also would have loved to have more focus on the dwarves individually. Viewing the Appendices, you can see just how much detail and depth they went into creating individual personalities for every dwarf, but I didn't feel like enough of that went into the film. Yeah, they're still more fleshed out than they were in the book overall, but just knowing how much work they put in to making each dwarf a singular character, it made me want even more for them to be more individualized. However, considering the sheer numbers, I do have to commend them for the job that they did, because that's an extremely hard thing to do. Doesn't mean I don't wish they could've been fleshed out slightly more, though, especially in the extended edition (which they were, but I wanted even more =P).<br /><br />I still wish the dwarves could've had their colored hoods and instruments, I still wish the troll scene could have been a little closer to the book (though I do like some of the changes they made), I still wish the stone giants were just in the distance (though this doesn't bother me too much at all), I still wish the tree scene at the end would've been different . . . but overall I do love the film, especially the extended edition.<br /><br />I have yet to watch the film commentaries, but I suspect I will soon enough--the Appendices, as I've said, were really great to watch, though.<br /><br />Part 7 (which is actually two disks, instead of the usual one--parts 1-6 were covered in LOTR) is titled \\"A Long-Expected Journey\\" and covers a lot of pre-production; includes a great introduction where Jackson explains how the film came to be and how he came to be director once again; the actors' boot camp and various other training; the shooting of the film and the various sets; pick-up shooting; and more.<br /><br />Part 8, \\"Return to Middle-Earth\\" further details the development, design, and production of The Hobbit, and explores in-depth the background of the main characters and the casting for said characters. There's a section on creating the dwarves as a whole, and how Jackson wanted to do for the dwarves what Tolkien did for the elves, and really go in-depth to their past and history and life. It also shows the concepts, creation, and design of the various sets (both physical and digital), and lastly, there's a look at the songs of The Hobbit.<br /><br />The first disk of the movie also has \\"New Zealand: Home of Middle-Earth\\" which is a cool look into the various places in NZ that were used for the film.<br /><br />I always loved watching the Appendices to LOTR, and The Hobbit was no different. I would definitely recommend this five-disk set--the extended edition of the film is amazing, and all the special features/appendices are just really great and interesting, too.<br /><br />Original Film Review Here: [...]<br />Gallery of Product Images Here: [...]

0375842209|5.0|When I saw the movie version of The Book Thief was coming out I thought it was the perfect time to finally read my copy of the book that has been sitting on my shelf for some time. With all the lovely reviews I have read I was sure I would love it as well and I was definitely not disappointed. While I cannot say that it is a feel good read in any way it is an incredibly beautiful story and one who's characters I will never forget.<br /><br />First and foremost the narrator of this incredible story is death himself. He's not the usual scythe and darkness death we have seen before but a death who is just doing his job and trying not to get too distracted by the strange lives of the living he passes by. There is a heart beating in that cold chest, one that cannot turn away from our main character, Liesel Meminger, a girl who refuses to let this horrid life she has been born into break her spirit. It might sound strange but, with all the war stories I have read in my lifetime, it is this book narrated by death that presents the most humane and heartbreaking story yet.<br /><br />Liesel is another wholly unique character. Made to go live with foster parents when the Communist label attached to her family becomes too dangerous, Liesel's younger brother dies on the way to their new home. Finding a book in the snow by her brother's grave, Liesel steals the book and resolves to learn how to read it. It is with this first theft that Liesel resolves to steal words when the world takes things from her. However words become so much more to our young heroine: a bridge connecting her to her foster Papa, Hans Hubermann; a way to escape the horrors happening around her, both figuratively and literally; a way to emotionally relate and communicate to the young Jewish man the Hubermanns hide in their basement. For someone who love words as well I became very attached to Liesel and her various ways of dealing with the unpredictable, often hypocritical world she had to try and navigate. How else might a young German girl, a member of the local Hitler Young group, deal with the unwarranted hatred she sees being thrown at Jewish people like the young man in their basement she has come to love like family?<br /><br />I couldn't end this review without mentioning the other unforgettable characters that saturate this story. By far my favorite character is Liesel's best friend, Rudy Steiner, a young boy always willing to do what his heart feels is right, regardless of the danger it might present for himself, and a boy who wants nothing more than a kiss from Liesel. I don't want to give too much away regarding their sweet, innocent relationship but I will advise you prepare and have some tissues when you begin reading the last 50 pages or so of the story. Another unforgettable character is Papa, a man who seems to have unlimited amounts of kindness and another character who refuses to let the propaganda of the Fuhrer dement what he knows to be the right way to be. There are so many more remarkable characters - Max, the young Jew in the basement, Liesel's harsh yet caring Mama, Rosa, the many colorful characters inhabiting Himmel Street - and this collection of humanity makes the ending of the book that much more poignant. I've never quite read anything like it.<br /><br />Anyone who loves reading history from an alternative viewpoint, especially history dealing with WWII, would be remise not to read this book. This is a powerful, beautiful, bittersweet story. It's the kind of story that you will not only never forget but one that, once you have read it, will make you forget what it was like to have not known and loved these characters. Truly a life changing story.How else might a young German girl, a member of the local Hitler Young group, deal with the unwarranted hatred she sees being thrown at Jewish people like the young man in their basement she has come to love like family?<br /><br />I couldn't end this review without mentioning the other unforgettable characters that saturate this story. 159514188X|4.0|&#34;Thirteen Reasons Why&#34; by Jay Asher is the story of Clay who receives a set of tapes. As he turns them on, the voice of Hannah (the girl he had a crush on, who recently committed suicide) can be heard detailing the 13 reasons why she killed herself. In the style of a chain letter, each individual she holds accountable will get a chance to hear these tapes. I found this book and it's concept so intriguing that I had to read it the moment the book arrived.<br /><br />The book was very well written and the story kept me turning the page. It did what I think the author was trying to accomplish.  This was to get us thinking about this young girl who, in my opinion, represents so many emotionally damaged young people out there who come to a point where they feel utterly helpless, devastated and so disappointed with this world that they take their own life.  And after the fact, those around them like Clay have their 'aha' moment where they realize, &#34;I should have seen the signs, they were so obvious, yet I missed them.&#34;<br /><br />As I was reading this, I kept shaking the book as if somehow I could shake Hannah and tell her that those were stupid reasons to kill herself.  I would tell her that there was no reason good enough that would justify ending her life.  And also that every problem has a solution.<br />But, then I realized that she was in pain, lost, depressed, feeling alone and betrayed.  At that age, the things that adults see as minor are magnified in the eyes of young people who are barely trying to set their path in the world.<br /><br />I encourage you to read this book even if you don’t find all the reasons believable, because too many of those reasons are as real as they get. And what some consider insignificant and harmless will never be insignificant to someone who is already spiraling down the black hole of depression.<br /><br />I really liked this book  a lot, so I gave it 4 and half kisses.<br /><br />For more reviews like this visit PattysKiss on YoutubePattysKiss on Youtube
